الحجة الثالثة : مزاولة الصنائع العقلية تدل على أن الإنسان قد يتعارض عنده دلايلان في مسألة عقلية بحيث يعجز عن القدح فى كل واحد منهما ، إما عجزا دائما أو فى بعض الأحوال . والعجز لا يتحقق إلا عند كونه مضطراً إلى اعتقاد صحة جميع المقدمات التي في الدليلين ، ولا شك أن واحدا منهما خطأ ، وإلا لصدق النقيضان ، وهذا يدل على أن البديهة قد تجزم بما لا يجوز الجزم به .
الحجة الرابعة : قد يكون الإنسان جازما بصحة جميع مقدمات دليل معين ثم يتبين له خطأ في بعض تلك المقدمات ولأجل ذلك ينتقل الرجل من مذهب إلى مذهب فجزمه بصحة تلك المقدمة الباطلة باطل فظهر أن البديهية متهمة (٢)
الحجة الخامسة : أنا نو نرى لاختلاف الأمزجة والعادات تأثيرا في الاعتقادات وذلك يقدح في البديهيات أما الأمزجة غلان ضيف المزاج يستقيح الإيلام وغليظ المزاج القاسي القلب قد يستحسنه . فرب إنسان يستحسن شيئا ويستقبحه غيره . وأما العادات فهو أن الإنسان إذا مارس كلمات الفلاسفة وألفها من أول عمره إلى آخره ربما صار بحيث يقطع بصحة كل ما يقولونه وبفساد كل ما يقول مخالفوهم ، ومن مارس كلام المتكلمين كان الأمر بالعكس. وكذا القول فى أرباب الملل فإن المسلم المقلد يستقبح كلام اليهودى فى أول الوهلة واليهودى بالعكس ، وما ذاك إلا بسبب العادات، وإذا ثبت أن لاختلاف الأمزجة والعادات أثرا في الجزم بما لا يجب الجزم به ، فلعل الجزم بهذه البديهيات مزاج عام أو لإلف عام وعلى هذا القدير لا يجب الوثوق . لا يقال : إن الإنسان يفرض نفسه خالية من مقتضيات الأمزجة والعادات فما يجزم العقل به فى تلك الحالة كان حقا ، لأن الجازم به فى هذه الحالة فهو صريح العقل لا المزاج والمادة . لانا نقول : هب أنا فرضنا خلو النفس على المزاج والعادة لكن فرض الخلو لا يوجب حصول الخلو ، فلملنا[؟] : هذا الكلام و الدليل على أن القدح في الضروريات بما أورده من الاحتمالات لاتؤثر في
(1) أقول جزم العقل أصلا.
(٢) أقول : قصور أفهام بعض الناس عن التمييز بين الحق والباطل واعتمادهم على ما يتقلدونه من آبائهم وأساتذتهم بموجب حسن ظنهم فيهم ليس بقادح في الأوليات ، وأيضا التشكك في النظريات ليست تعارض الدليلين أو النقل من مذهب إلى مذهب بسبب ترجح أحد دليلين متعارضين لا يقدح في النظريات ، وصناعة المنطق لا سيما صناعة سوفسطيقا منه إنما بين لإرشاد العقلاء إلى طريق الحقي ومجانبة ما يقتضى الضلال في العقائد والمباحث النظرية .
The third argument: Engaging in intellectual crafts indicates that a person may have two conflicting proofs in a rational matter such that he is unable to refute either, either permanently or in some states. Inability only occurs when he is compelled to believe the truth of all the premises in both proofs, and there is no doubt that one of them is false, otherwise the two contradictories would be true. This indicates that self-evident [judgment] may affirm what ought not to be affirmed.
The fourth argument: A person may be convinced of the truth of all premises of a specific proof, then an error in some of those premises becomes clear to him, and for that reason a man moves from one school to another. So his conviction of the truth of that false premise is false, so it appears that self-evidence is suspect.
The fifth argument: We see that differences in temperaments and habits have an effect on beliefs, and that impugns self-evident [truths]. As for temperaments: a delicate temperament considers causing pain repulsive, while a rough, hard-hearted temperament may consider it good. So one person may consider something good and another consider it bad. As for habits: a person who has practiced the words of philosophers and become accustomed to them from his early life to its end may come to be such that he is certain of the truth of everything they say and the falsity of everything their opponents say. One who has practiced the speech of theologians has the opposite. Likewise with the followers of religions: a Muslim imitator rejects the speech of a Jew at first glance, and a Jew the opposite. That is only due to habits. If it is established that differences in temperaments and habits have an effect on affirmation of what ought not to be affirmed, then perhaps the affirmation of these self-evident [truths] is a general temperament or a general habit, and on this basis trust is not obligatory. It is not said: 'A person can suppose himself free from the influences of temperaments and habits, and what the intellect affirms in that state is true, because the affirmer in that state is pure reason, not temperament and matter.' We say: Suppose we assume the soul free from temperament and habit, but the assumption of freedom does not bring about actual freedom. So we [say] [?]: This speech and the proof that the impugning of necessary [truths] by the probabilities he adduced does not affect...
(1) I say: The judgment of reason is fundamental.
(2) I say: The limited understanding of some people in distinguishing truth from falsehood and their reliance on what they imitate from their fathers and teachers due to their good opinion of them does not impugn first principles. Also, skepticism in theoretical matters, whether conflict of two proofs or moving from one school to another due to the preponderance of one of two conflicting proofs, does not impugn theoretical [truths]. The art of logic, especially the art of sophistry, is only intended to guide rational persons to the path of truth and to avoid what necessitates error in beliefs and theoretical investigations.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments from skeptics against self-evident truths, followed by a refutation. The first argument (third) is about conflicting evidence: a person may have two compelling arguments that contradict, forcing belief in all premises even though one must be false, showing self-evidence can lead to false conviction. The second argument (fourth) is about changing one's mind: a person convinced of all premises of an argument later discovers an error, so his initial conviction was false, making self-evidence suspect. The third argument (fifth) is about influence of temperament and habit: different temperaments judge differently; habit makes people accept philosophical or religious doctrines; thus self-evident judgments may be products of temperament or habit. The skeptics anticipate an objection: one can imagine oneself free from these influences, then reason's judgment is pure. They respond: that assumption does not bring about actual freedom. The marginal notes clarify: the first note says reason's judgment is fundamental; the second note says that limited understanding and imitation by some people does not impugn first principles, and that dialectical conflict or school change does not invalidate theoretical truths; logic and sophistry were developed to guide to truth.

The Skeptics' Dilemma Against Self-Evident Truths
Razi presents the skeptics' dilemma against self-evident truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the skeptics' dilemma that attempts to undermine self-evident truths by showing that either response leads to a problem.

Core Idea
The skeptics force a choice that seems to make self-evident truths dependent on theoretical reasoning.

The skeptics have gathered their arguments against self-evident truths. Now they present a clever dilemma to their opponents: any response you give, or lack thereof, undermines the very foundation of self-evident truths. This passage explains that dilemma and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two-part structure introduced by 'إما أن ... أو ...'.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two options: either answer the skeptics' arguments or do not answer. Find these two possibilities in the text.
3
Understand the first horn (answering)
The skeptics claim that answering leads to circularity because it requires theoretical reasoning, making self-evident truths dependent on theoretical truths.
The key phrase is 'هذا خلف' (this is circular).
4
Understand the second horn (not answering)
If you do not answer, the doubt remains, and it is self-evident that certainty in self-evident truths is not permissible while doubt persists.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The skeptics conclude that in both cases, self-evident truths are impugned ('فقد توجه القدح فى البديهيات على كلا التقديرين').
Consider the strength of this dilemma: it seems to leave no escape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا مجموع أدلة الطاعنين فى البديهيات . ثم قالوا لخصومهم : إما أن تشتغلوا بالجواب عما ذكرنا أو لا تشتغلوا به فإن اشتغلتم بالجواب حصل غرضنا لأنكم حينئذ تكونون معترفين بأن الإقرار بالبديهيات لا يصفر عن الشوائب إلا بالجواب عن هذه الإشكالات، ولا شك أن الجواب عنها لا يحل إلا بدقيق النظر والموقوف على النظرى أولى بأن يكون نظريا ، فكانت البديهيات مفتقرة إلى النظريات المستقرة إلى البديهيات هذا خلف. وإن لم تشتغلوا بالجواب بقيت الشبهة المذكورة خالية عن الجواب ، ومن المعلوم بالبديهة أن مع بقائها لا يحل الجزم بالبديهيات فقد توجه القدح فى البديهيات على كلا التقديرين (٢)
This is the totality of the arguments of the attackers against self-evident truths. Then they said to their opponents: Either you engage in answering what we have mentioned, or you do not engage in it. If you engage in answering, our purpose is achieved because you will then be acknowledging that affirmation of self-evident truths is not free from defects except by answering these objections; and there is no doubt that answering them can only be resolved by precise reflection, and what is dependent on theoretical [reasoning] is more fitting to be theoretical. So self-evident truths would be dependent on theoretical [truths] which themselves depend on self-evident truths—this is circular. If you do not engage in answering, the mentioned doubt remains unanswered, and it is self-evident that with its persistence, certainty in self-evident truths is not permissible. So the impugning of self-evident truths is established on both suppositions.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage summarizes the skeptics' position and presents a dilemma to their opponents. The skeptics say: either you answer our arguments or you don't. If you answer, you admit that self-evident truths are not pure and need defense, and since the defense relies on theoretical reasoning, self-evident truths become dependent on theoretical truths, which in turn depend on self-evident truths—a circularity. If you don't answer, the doubt remains and your self-evident truths remain uncertain. Thus in either case, self-evident truths are undermined. The marginal note (2) appears at the end and may be a reference or comment, but the text itself presents the dilemma as a conclusive argument.

خلف
تقديرين

Misconception: One might think that answering skeptical arguments reinforces self-evident truths, but the skeptics claim it reveals their dependence on theory.
Correction: The skeptics argue that answering actually shows that self-evident truths are not self-sufficient but require theoretical support, leading to circularity.

Checkpoint

B) The doubt remains and self-evident truths remain uncertain
C) Self-evident truths become stronger
D) The opponents admit circularity

Key Takeaway
The skeptics claim that no matter how you respond, self-evident truths are impugned, either through circularity or persistent doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

In your own life, have you ever felt stuck between defending your beliefs and letting doubts linger? What would it mean for you to truly settle that tension, not just intellectually, but in your heart?

The Sophist Dilemma Against Self-Evident Truths
The sophists argue that self-evident truths require defense, creating a dilemma for opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sophist dilemma that challenges the self-evident nature of basic truths and why it creates a circularity problem.

Core Idea
The sophists force a choice: defend self-evident truths with theory (circular) or leave them undefended (doubtful).

Imam Razi presents a powerful skeptical argument against self-evident truths (البديهيات). The sophists claim that these truths cannot be taken for granted; they require defense. But defending them leads to a dilemma that seems to undermine their self-evidence. This passage shows the sophists' challenge and the logical trap they set for those who affirm self-evident truths.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation to get the overall flow of the sophist argument.
The passage contains a complex hypothetical about the soul's dispositions.
2
Understand the initial argument
The sophists argue that the soul cannot be supposed to be empty of cognitive faculties, and even if it could, there may be unknown temperamental dispositions that prevent certainty from being purely rational.
This sets up the basis for the doubt.
3
Identify the dilemma
The sophists present a dilemma: either opponents answer the objections, or they do not. Both options have damaging consequences.
This is the core of the sophist challenge.
4
Analyze the two branches
If opponents answer, they admit that self-evident truths need theoretical defense, leading to circularity (theoretical depends on self-evident, which depends on theoretical). If they do not answer, the doubt remains, and certainty is impossible.
This shows the dilemma's force.
5
Reflect on the challenge
Consider how a defender of self-evident truths might respond without falling into the dilemma. This reflection prepares you for later kalam responses.
The challenge is not a refutation but a test of coherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن فرضنا خلو النفس عنه [؟] ما لكنها ما خلت عنهما ، وحينئذ يكون الجزم بسبها لا بسبب العقل . سلمنا أن فرض الخلو يوجب الخلو ، ليكن لعل في نفوسنا من الهيئات المزاجية والعادية ما لا نعرفه على التفصيل ، وحينئذ لا يمكننا فرض خلو النفس عنهما وذلك سبب التهمة . فهذا مجموع أدلة الطاعنين فى البديهيات . ثم قالوا لخصومهم : إما أن تشتغلوا بالجواب عما ذكرنا أو لا تشتغلوا به فإن اشتغلتم بالجواب حصل غرضنا لأنكم حينئذ تكونون معترفين بأن الإقرار بالبديهيات لا يصفر عن الشوائب إلا بالجواب عن هذه الإشكالات، ولا شك أن الجواب عنها لا يحل إلا بدقيق النظر والموقوف على النظرى أولى بأن يكون نظريا ، فكانت البديهيات مفتقرة إلى النظريات المستقرة إلى البديهيات هذا خلف. وإن لم تشتغلوا بالجواب بقيت الشبهة المذكورة خالية عن الجواب ، ومن المعلوم بالبديهة أن مع بقائها لا يحل الجزم بالبديهيات فقد توجه القدح فى البديهيات على كلا التقديرين
And if we suppose the soul to be empty of it, [?] it was not empty of both, and then certainty about it is not due to reason. We grant that supposing emptiness necessitates emptiness, but perhaps within our souls there are temperamental and habitual dispositions that we do not know in detail, and then we cannot suppose the soul empty of both, and that is the cause of suspicion. So this is the sum of the arguments of the critics of self-evident truths. Then they said to their opponents: Either you engage in answering what we have mentioned or you do not engage in it. If you engage in answering, our purpose is achieved because then you are admitting that assent to self-evident truths is not free from defects except by answering these objections, and there is no doubt that answering them is only achieved by precise reasoning, and what depends on the theoretical is more deserving of being theoretical, so self-evident truths would be dependent on theoretical matters that depend on self-evident truths—this is circular. And if you do not engage in answering, the aforementioned doubt remains unanswered, and it is self-evident that with its remaining, certainty in self-evident truths is not achieved, so criticism of self-evident truths is established on both assumptions.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the sophist attack on self-evident truths (بديهيات) and the dilemma they pose to those who affirm them. The author (Imam Razi) reports the sophist argument: if we try to conceive the soul without any cognitive faculties, we find it is not empty, so certainty arises from something other than reason. Even if we grant that supposing emptiness leads to emptiness, there may be unknown internal dispositions preventing that supposition, causing suspicion. The sophists then present a dilemma: if opponents answer the objections, they admit that self-evident truths require theoretical defense, making them dependent on theory (which itself depends on self-evident truths, leading to circularity). If they do not answer, the doubt remains, and certainty is impossible. Thus, self-evident truths are undermined in both cases.

Key Terms
البديهيات
النظريات
الدور
الشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the sophist dilemma proves that self-evident truths are false or invalid.
Correction: The dilemma does not disprove self-evident truths; it only challenges their status by showing a circularity if defended and doubt if not defended. A proper response must avoid both horns.

Checkpoint
What is the sophist dilemma concerning self-evident truths?
A) They are either false or uncertain.
B) Opponents must either answer objections (admitting dependence on theory) or remain silent (leaving doubt).
C) Self-evident truths are impossible because they require proof.
D) The soul cannot be empty, so all knowledge is innate.

Key Takeaway
The sophist dilemma challenges self-evident truths by creating a circularity if defended and uncertainty if not, but it does not disprove them; a proper kalam response must address this challenge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your most basic beliefs (like the existence of truth or your own consciousness) are being attacked by doubts you cannot answer? How did you respond? In light of this lesson, what is the danger of either trying to 'prove' them or ignoring the doubts?
?
The sophists claim that defending self-evident truths leads to circular reasoning. Reflect on a time you tried to defend a foundational belief (e.g., 'the Quran is true') and ended up in a circular argument. What did you learn from that experience? Are you willing to admit that some truths are simply known without proof?
?
If you were faced with a sophisticated skeptic who presented this dilemma, would you feel confident in your knowledge of self-evident truths? Why or why not? What is the one thing from this lesson that should change how you respond to such challenges?

Defending Self-Evident Truths Against Sophist Doubts
The commentator refutes the sophist arguments, asserting the immunity of self-evident truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the immunity of self-evident truths from sophist skepticism, using two distinct arguments.

Core Idea
Self-evident truths remain certain in sound minds, unaffected by sophist doubts.

In this passage, the commentator confronts the sophist challenge that questions the foundation of all knowledge. He offers two powerful responses: first, that external influences like nature and habit do not shake the truth recognized by all rational people; second, that the sophist's doubt, even if unanswered, cannot undermine the certainty of self-evident truths. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two parts: the first response about natures, habits, and religions; the second response about why not answering the doubt does not harm self-evident truths.
The text uses technical terms like 'البديهيات' (self-evident truths) and 'الأوليات' (primary truths).
2
Identify the first argument
The commentator argues that while natural dispositions, habits, and religious norms influence beliefs, they do not oppose the firm truth that all rational people recognize. He even includes the foolish, children, and madmen to show the universality of this recognition.
He references a saying about the three heads of devils: nature, habit, and examples.
3
Identify the second argument
The commentator explains that not engaging in a refutation does not mean the doubt remains effective. Sound minds have certainty that is not shaken by sophist doubts. The sophists themselves only aim to cast doubt, not to prove the falsehood of self-evident truths.
He anticipates and neutralizes a potential objection that the sophist doubt might be self-refuting.
4
Reflect on the overall defense
Think about how both arguments together protect the foundation of knowledge. The first sets limits on what can influence truth, and the second shows that doubt without certainty has no real power.
Ask yourself: Why does the commentator emphasize that even the foolish and insane agree on these truths?

أقول أما استحسان الأشياء واستقباحها فيجى القول فيها ، وأما مقتضيات الطبائع والعادات والديانات فلا شك في كونها مؤثرة في اعتقادات الدوام لكنها لا تعارض متانة الحق الذي يعترف به جميع المقلاء حتى البله والصبيان والمجانين ، وقد حذر العلماء طالبي الحق عن متابعة الأهواء والطبائع والعادات مثل قول القائل رؤساء الشياطين ثلاثة شوائب الطبيعة ووساوس العادة ونواميس الأمثلة ، ولا شك أن البديهيات لا تقدح بها . أقول : عدم الاشتغال بالجواب لا يقتضى بقاء الشبرة القادحة في الأوليات ، فإنها مع جزم العقل غير مؤثرة في العقول السليمة ، بل إنما لا يشتغل بالجواب لفقدان ما يتفقون عليه من مبادىء الإيجاب ولكون الأوليات مستغنية عن الذب عنها بالحجج والبينات . لا يقال في جوابهم : إن شهتكم التي أوردة، وها ليست قضايا حسية فهي إما بديهيات وإما نظريات مستندة إلى بديهيات ، فلو كانت قادمة فى البديهيات لكانت قادحة في أنفسها . لأنهم يقولون : نحن لم نقصد فى إيراد هذه الشبهة إبطال البديهيات باليقين بل قصدنا إيقاع الشك فيها وكيف ما كان مقصودنا حاصل .
I say: As for the approval and disapproval of things, discussion may be had about them. As for the dictates of natures, habits, and religions, there is no doubt that they are influential in beliefs of permanence, but they do not oppose the firmness of truth which all rational people recognize, even the foolish, children, and madmen. Scholars have warned seekers of truth against following whims, natures, and habits, such as the saying: 'The heads of devils are three: impurities of nature, whispers of habit, and laws of examples.' There is no doubt that self-evident truths are not undermined by them. I say: Not engaging in the answer does not necessitate the persistence of the doubt that criticizes primary truths, for they, with the certainty of reason, are not influential in sound minds. Rather, one does not engage in the answer due to the lack of agreed-upon principles of affirmation and because primary truths are independent of defense by proofs and evidences. It should not be said in response to them: 'Your doubt that you have mentioned is not a sensory proposition; it is either self-evident or theoretical based on self-evident truths. If it were critical of self-evident truths, it would be critical of itself.' Because they say: 'We did not intend by presenting this doubt to refute self-evident truths with certainty, but we intended to cast doubt upon them, and however it is, our purpose is achieved.'
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage contains two responses from the commentator (likely al-Razi himself or a later scholar) to the sophist attack. The first response distinguishes between innate dispositions (like aesthetic judgments) and the dictates of nature, habit, and religion, which may influence beliefs but do not conflict with the firm truth acknowledged by all rational individuals, including the foolish and insane. The second response argues that not answering the sophist does not mean the doubt remains effective, because sound minds hold self-evident truths with certainty that is unaffected by such doubts. The commentator also anticipates a potential rejoinder that the sophist objection might be self-refuting, but notes that the sophists claim they only aim to cast doubt, not to achieve certainty.

الأوليات
الشبهة
العقول السليمة

Misconception: Some might think that if a scholar does not respond to a doubt, it means the doubt is valid and self-evident truths are uncertain.
Correction: The commentator explains that not responding does not imply the doubt is effective; self-evident truths are certain in sound minds regardless.

Checkpoint

A) Self-evident truths require theoretical proof.
B) The sophist objection is itself a self-evident truth.
C) Sound minds remain certain of self-evident truths despite the doubt.
D) The sophist must be physically punished to admit truth.

Key Takeaway
The commentator defends self-evident truths by showing that external influences do not upset them and that unanswered sophist doubts cannot shake the certainty of sound minds.

Proof that Theoretical Reasoning Yields Knowledge
Author states his thesis and gives a proof from two certain premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that thought (fikr) can produce certain knowledge based on the combination of certain premises.

Core Idea
Thought yields knowledge when certain premises necessitate a conclusion.

The author begins by establishing the possibility of theoretical reasoning. He notes opposing views and then presents a concise proof: if two certain premises are present together in the mind and necessitate a conclusion, then thought exists. This lesson unpacks that proof and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: the author first states his thesis, then mentions opposing views, then gives his proof.
The proof is very short; pay attention to the logical connection between premises and conclusion.
2
Identify the core argument
The author says: 'Our proof: Each one of the premises of the mentioned example is certain, and they can both be present in the mind in a combination that necessitates the conclusion. Thus, thought that yields knowledge exists.' Recognize that this is a syllogistic argument.
The 'mentioned example' likely refers to a standard syllogism not explicitly given here.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this proof matters. The author is defending the possibility of certain knowledge through reasoning against those who deny it entirely or restrict it to mathematics.
This proof serves as a foundation for the rest of the work.

مسألة : الفكر المفيد للعلم موجود

والسمنية أنكروه مطلقا ، وجمع من المهندسين اعترفوا به في المدديات والهندسيات وأنكروه في الإلهيات، وزعموا أن المقصد الأقصى فيها الأخذ بالأولى والأخلق ، أما الجزم فلا سبيل إليه .
لنا : أن كل واحد من مقدمة المثال المذكورة يقينى وقد يجتمعان في الذهن اجتماعا مستلزما للنتيجة المذكورة ، فالنظر المفيد للعلم موجود .
احتج المنكرون للنظر مطلقا بأمور أربعة .
أولها : العلم بأن الاعتقاد الحاصل عقيب النظر علم لا يجوز أن يكون ضروريا إذ كثيرا ينكشف الأمر بخلافه، ولا نظريا وإلا لزم التسلسل وهو محال .
ثانيها : أن المطلوب إن كان معلوما فلا فائدة فى طلبه ، وإلا فإذا وجده كيف يعرف أنه مطلوبه .
وثالثها : أن الإنسان قد يكون مصرا على صحة دليل زمانا مديدا ثم يظهر له بعد ذلك ضعفه بدليل ثان ، وذلك الاحتمال قائم فى ذلك الثانى ، ومع قيام الاحتمال لا يحصل النعين .
رابعها : أن العلم بالمقدمتين لا يحصل معا في الذهن بدليل أنا نجد في أنفسنا أنا متى وجهنا الذهن نحو استحضار معلوم نقدر عليه فى تلك الحالة توجيهه نحو استحضار معلوم آخر ، فالحاضر في الذهن أبدا ليس إلا العلم بمقدمة واحدة وذلك غير منتج بالاتفاق فالفكر لا يفيد العلم .
Question: The thought that yields knowledge exists.
The Samaniyya denied it absolutely, and a group of engineers acknowledged it in numerical and geometrical matters but denied it in theology, claiming the ultimate goal there is to take the better and more fitting; as for certainty, there is no way to it.
Our proof: Each one of the premises of the mentioned example is certain, and they can both be present in the mind in a combination that necessitates the mentioned conclusion. Thus, the thought that yields knowledge exists.
The deniers of thought absolutely argued with four matters:
First: The knowledge that the belief obtained after thought is knowledge cannot be necessary, since often the matter is revealed contrary to it; nor theoretical, because that would lead to infinite regress, which is impossible.
Second: If the sought is known, then no benefit in seeking it; otherwise, when he finds it, how does he know it is his sought?
Third: A person may insist on the validity of a proof for a long time, then later its weakness appears to him through a second proof; and that probability exists in the second, and with the existence of probability, certainty does not occur.
Fourth: The knowledge of both premises cannot be obtained together in the mind, as we find in ourselves that when we direct the mind to recall one known thing, we are capable at that time of directing it to recall another known thing. So what is present in the mind at any time is only knowledge of one premise, and that is unproductive by agreement. Thus, thought does not yield knowledge.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents the proof that theoretical reasoning (fikr) can yield knowledge. The author first notes that the Samaniyya deny it entirely, while some engineers accept it only in mathematics but not in theology. The author's proof is simple: if we have two certain premises that together necessitate a conclusion, then the reasoning process exists. Then four objections are listed without immediate refutation. The first objection argues that the belief after reasoning cannot be necessary (often mistaken) nor theoretical (infinite regress). The second objection is a version of Meno's paradox: if the conclusion is already known, seeking is pointless; if unknown, how can it be recognized? The third objection points to fallibilism: a proof once accepted can later be overturned. The fourth objection claims that only one premise can be in the mind at a time, so no conjunction of premises occurs. These are classic skeptical challenges to the possibility of rational inference.

Key Terms
فكر
علم
نظر
يقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that theoretical reasoning cannot produce certain knowledge at all.
Correction: The author shows that when premises are certain and their combination necessitates a conclusion, the reasoning process yields certain knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the author's proof, what is required for thought to yield knowledge?
A) The premises must be probable
B) The premises must be certain and their combination must necessitate the conclusion
C) The premises must be from geometry
D) The conclusion must be known beforehand

Key Takeaway
The existence of certain knowledge through reasoning is proven by the fact that certain premises can necessitate a conclusion when combined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you demand the same level of certainty as you would for a mathematical proof, or do you settle for 'better and more fitting'? What would it take for you to seek certainty in every belief?
?
The Samaniyya denied that thought can yield knowledge. Have you ever doubted that your reasoning about God can lead to certainty? How does the author's proof challenge that doubt, and what will you do to act on that challenge today?
?
Are you content with plausible beliefs about your Creator, or will you commit to learning the proofs that lead to certainty? What specific step will you take this week to replace a guess with a certain conviction?

Four Objections to Theoretical Knowledge
Author lists four objections from deniers of thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four classical skeptical objections against the possibility of achieving certain knowledge through reasoning.

Core Idea
Skeptics raise four challenges: infinite regress, Meno's paradox, fallibility, and mental incompatibility.

The deniers of thought present four arguments to show that reasoning cannot yield certainty. These objections challenge the very notion of rational inference. This lesson presents each objection clearly, as reported by the author, setting the stage for their refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full list of four objections. Note that they are enumerated and each is a self-contained argument.
The author reports these objections; he will answer them later.
2
Examine the first objection
The first objection: the belief after thought cannot be necessary (because often mistaken) nor theoretical (leads to infinite regress). Understand the dilemma.
This objection deals with the classification of knowledge.
3
Examine the second objection
The second objection is a version of Meno's paradox: if the sought is known, no need to seek; if unknown, how recognize it? Grasp the problem of inquiry.
4
Examine the third and fourth objections
Third: proofs can later be overturned, so no certainty. Fourth: only one premise can be in the mind at a time, so no conjunction. Understand each.
The fourth objection is about mental capacity.

The Existence of Beneficial Reasoning
Sanusi states the issue and provides a brief proof for beneficial reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that reasoning (al-fikr al-mufid) can produce certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Reasoning yields knowledge when two certain premises entail a certain conclusion.

The author begins this section by affirming that thought which yields knowledge does exist. He contrasts this with two groups of deniers: the Samaniyya who deny it entirely, and some mathematicians who accept it in arithmetic but deny it in theology. He then provides a concise proof based on the logical structure of valid inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: statement of issue, mention of opponents, proof.
2
Identify the proof
Identify the two premises mentioned and the conclusion. Understand how the author claims they are certain and their conjunction entails the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the opponents
Note the two groups: the Samaniyya and the mathematicians. They deny either all reasoning or reasoning in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الفكر المفيد للعلم موجود
والسمنية أنكروه مطلقا، وجمع من المهندسين اعترفوا به في العدديات والهندسيات وأنكروه في الإلهيات، وزعموا أن المقصد الأقصى فيها الأخذ بالأولى والأليق، أما الجزم فلا سبيل إليه.
لنا: أن كل واحد من مقدمة المثال المذكورة يقيني وقد يجتمعان في الذهن اجتماعا مستلزما للنتيجة المذكورة، فالنظر المفيد للعلم موجود.
احتج المنكرون للنظر مطلقا بأمور أربعة:
أولها: العلم بأن الاعتقاد الحاصل عقيب النظر علم لا يجوز أن يكون ضروريا إذ كثيرا ينكشف الأمر بخلافه، ولا نظريا وإلا لزم التسلسل وهو محال.
ثانيها: أن المطلوب إن كان معلوما فلا فائدة في طلبه، وإلا فإذا وجده كيف يعرف أنه مطلوبه.
ثالثها: أن الإنسان قد يكون مصرا على صحة دليل زمانا مديدا ثم يظهر له بعد ذلك ضعفه بدليل ثان، وذلك الاحتمال قائم في ذلك الثاني، ومع قيام الاحتمال لا يحصل اليقين.
رابعها: أن العلم بالمقدمتين لا يحصل معا في الذهن بدليل أنا نجد في أنفسنا أنا متى وجهنا الذهن نحو استحضار معلوم نقدر عليه في تلك الحالة توجيهه نحو استحضار معلوم آخر، فالحاضر في الذهن أبدا ليس إلا العلم بمقدمة واحدة وذلك غير منتج بالاتفاق فالفكر لا يفيد العلم.
احتج المنكرون النظر في الإلهيات بوجهين:
أحدهما: أن إمكان طلب التصديق موقوف على تصور الموضوع والمحمول والحقائق الإلهية غير متصورة. لنا: ما سبق أنا لا نتصور إلا ما نجده بحواسنا أو نفوسنا أو عقولنا، وإذا فقد التصور الذي هو شرط التصديق امتنع التصديق أيضا.
الجواب عن الأول: أنه نظري والتسلسل عنه لازم، لأن لزوم النتيجة عن المقدمتين إذا كان ضروريا كانتا ضروريتين إما ابتداء أو بواسطة شأنها كذلك، وعنده علم ضروري بأن اللازم عن الضروري ضروري. علم بالضرورة أن الحاصل علم.
وعن الثاني: أنه معلوم التصور مجهول التصديق والمطلوب هو التصديق، فإذا وجده ميزه عن غيره بالتصور المعلوم.
وعن الثالث: أنه معارض بأغلاط الحس.
وعن الرابع: أنا قد نعقل القضية الشرطية وهي مركبة من جملتين، والحكم بلزوم إحدى الجانبين الأخرى يستدعي حضور العلم بهما حال الحكم بذلك اللزوم، وذلك يدل على إمكان اجتماع العلمين دفعة في الذهن.
ومن الخامس: هب أن تلك الماهيات غير متصورة بحسب حقائقها لكنها متصورة بحسب عوارضها المشتركة بينها وبين المحدثات وذلك كافٍ في إمكان التصديق.
وعن السادس: أن ما ذكرتموه يدل على صعوبة تحصيل هذا العلم لا على تعذره.
Issue: The thought that yields knowledge exists.
The Samaniyya denied it absolutely, and a group of the engineers acknowledged it in arithmetic and geometry but denied it in divine matters, claiming that the ultimate aim in them is to adopt the most appropriate and fitting, but certainty has no path to it.
Our proof: Each of the two premises of the mentioned example is certain, and they may come together in the mind in a conjunction that entails the mentioned conclusion. Thus, the reasoning that yields knowledge exists.
The deniers of reasoning absolutely objected with four things:
First: The knowledge that the belief attained after reasoning is such that it cannot be necessary, for often the matter is revealed to be contrary to it, nor theoretical, otherwise circularity would follow, which is impossible.
Second: If the sought is known, then there is no benefit in seeking it; but if it is not known, then when he finds it, how does he know that it is his sought?
Third: A person may be insistent on the correctness of a proof for a long time, then later its weakness appears to him through a second proof, and that possibility remains in the second one, and with the possibility remaining certainty is not obtained.
Fourth: Knowledge of the two premises does not occur together in the mind, evidenced by the fact that we find in ourselves that when we direct the mind toward summoning one known thing we are able to direct it toward summoning another, so what is present in the mind at any moment is only knowledge of one premise, and that is not productive by agreement, so thought does not yield knowledge.
The deniers of reasoning in divine matters objected with two arguments:
First: The possibility of seeking assent depends on conceiving the subject and predicate, and divine realities are not conceivable. [Objector says:] We have what preceded: we only conceive what we find through our senses, our selves, or our intellects, and when the conception which is a condition of assent is absent, assent is also impossible.
The answer to the first: It is theoretical, and circularity is not entailed because if the entailment of the conclusion from the two premises is necessary, then they are both necessary, either immediately or through an intermediary with the same property, and he has necessary knowledge that what is entailed by the necessary is necessary. It is known necessarily that what is obtained is knowledge.
And to the second: It is known in conception but unknown in assent, and the sought is assent, so when he finds it he distinguishes it from others by the known conception.
And to the third: It is countered by errors of the senses.
And to the fourth: We may understand a conditional proposition, which is composed of two statements, and the judgment of the entailment of one side from the other requires the presence of knowledge of both at the time of judging that entailment, and that indicates the possibility of the two knowledges coming together in the mind at once.
And to the fifth: Suppose those essences are not conceivable in terms of their true realities, but they are conceivable in terms of their common accidents between them and the contingent things, and that is sufficient for the possibility of assent.
And to the sixth: What you have mentioned indicates the difficulty of obtaining this knowledge, not its impossibility.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-refutation sequence. It begins by stating the issue (mas'ala) that 'thought that yields knowledge exists'. The Samaniyya (a sect) denied it altogether, while some mathematicians acknowledged it in mathematics but denied it in theology, claiming that only plausible opinions are possible there, not certainty. The author then gives a brief proof: if two certain premises come together in the mind in a way that entails a certain conclusion, that process yields knowledge. Next, he lists four objections from absolute deniers of reasoning: (1) the belief after reasoning is neither necessary nor theoretical (to avoid circularity); (2) if the sought is known, seeking is futile, if unknown, one cannot recognize it; (3) a long-held proof may later be shown false, so doubt persists; (4) the mind cannot hold both premises simultaneously, so no inference occurs. Then two objections from deniers of theological reasoning: (5) divine essences are inconceivable, so assent is impossible; (6) even the self (the 'I') is disputed, how then can we know the hidden? The author then provides six answers in order: to (1) he distinguishes necessary entailment from circularity; to (2) he distinguishes conception from assent; to (3) he notes that sensory errors also occur but do not invalidate all sense perception; to (4) he gives the example of conditional propositions requiring simultaneous knowledge; to (5) he admits that divine essences are not directly conceivable but are known through their attributes common with contingent things; to (6) he says this only shows difficulty, not impossibility.

Key Terms
الفكر المفيد
النظر
السمنية
المهندسين
المقدمتين
النتيجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some groups think that reasoning can never yield certainty, only opinion.
Correction: The author shows that when premises are certain and the inference is valid, the result is certain knowledge.

Checkpoint
What do the mathematicians (engineers) concede about reasoning?
A) They deny it absolutely.
B) They accept it in arithmetic and geometry but deny it in divine matters.
C) They accept it in all fields.
D) They deny it only in mathematics.

Key Takeaway
Beneficial reasoning exists when two certain premises entail a certain conclusion, contrary to the deniers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you demand certainty in your religious beliefs, or do you settle for 'fitting' opinions? Where in your life have you accepted plausibility without proof?
?
When you contemplate the existence of God, do you treat it as a mathematical certainty or a mere preference? What would change if you were as rigorous in divine matters as in your daily calculations?
?
The Samaniyya rejected all reasoning. Have you ever avoided intellectual engagement with faith out of fear or laziness? What would it cost you to take the proof seriously?

Four Objections from Absolute Deniers
Sanusi enumerates four objections from absolute deniers of reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main objections raised by those who deny that reasoning can yield knowledge at all.

Core Idea
Deniers argue that reasoning leads to circularity, futility, uncertainty, or simultaneous knowledge impossibility.

After stating his proof, the author presents the arguments of those who reject reasoning entirely. These four objections challenge the very possibility of obtaining knowledge through thought. Understanding them is crucial because they represent deep skepticism that must be addressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the four objections
Read the Arabic passage listing the four objections. Then read the translation. Notice each objection is a distinct logical problem.
2
Understand each objection
For each objection, identify the core claim: (1) is the result necessary or theoretical? (2) is the sought known or unknown? (3) can certainty survive later refutation? (4) can the mind hold two premises at once?
3
Connect to the proof
Reflect on how each objection targets the author's claim that reasoning yields knowledge. Note that the author will answer them later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الفكر المفيد للعلم موجود
والسمنية أنكروه مطلقا، وجمع من المهندسين اعترفوا به في العدديات والهندسيات وأنكروه في الإلهيات، وزعموا أن المقصد الأقصى فيها الأخذ بالأولى والأليق، أما الجزم فلا سبيل إليه.
لنا: أن كل واحد من مقدمة المثال المذكورة يقيني وقد يجتمعان في الذهن اجتماعا مستلزما للنتيجة المذكورة، فالنظر المفيد للعلم موجود.
احتج المنكرون للنظر مطلقا بأمور أربعة:
أولها: العلم بأن الاعتقاد الحاصل عقيب النظر علم لا يجوز أن يكون ضروريا إذ كثيرا ينكشف الأمر بخلافه، ولا نظريا وإلا لزم التسلسل وهو محال.
ثانيها: أن المطلوب إن كان معلوما فلا فائدة في طلبه، وإلا فإذا وجده كيف يعرف أنه مطلوبه.
ثالثها: أن الإنسان قد يكون مصرا على صحة دليل زمانا مديدا ثم يظهر له بعد ذلك ضعفه بدليل ثان، وذلك الاحتمال قائم في ذلك الثاني، ومع قيام الاحتمال لا يحصل اليقين.
رابعها: أن العلم بالمقدمتين لا يحصل معا في الذهن بدليل أنا نجد في أنفسنا أنا متى وجهنا الذهن نحو استحضار معلوم نقدر عليه في تلك الحالة توجيهه نحو استحضار معلوم آخر، فالحاضر في الذهن أبدا ليس إلا العلم بمقدمة واحدة وذلك غير منتج بالاتفاق فالفكر لا يفيد العلم.
احتج المنكرون النظر في الإلهيات بوجهين:
أحدهما: أن إمكان طلب التصديق موقوف على تصور الموضوع والمحمول والحقائق الإلهية غير متصورة. لنا: ما سبق أنا لا نتصور إلا ما نجده بحواسنا أو نفوسنا أو عقولنا، وإذا فقد التصور الذي هو شرط التصديق امتنع التصديق أيضا.
الجواب عن الأول: أنه نظري والتسلسل عنه لازم، لأن لزوم النتيجة عن المقدمتين إذا كان ضروريا كانتا ضروريتين إما ابتداء أو بواسطة شأنها كذلك، وعنده علم ضروري بأن اللازم عن الضروري ضروري. علم بالضرورة أن الحاصل علم.
وعن الثاني: أنه معلوم التصور مجهول التصديق والمطلوب هو التصديق، فإذا وجده ميزه عن غيره بالتصور المعلوم.
وعن الثالث: أنه معارض بأغلاط الحس.
وعن الرابع: أنا قد نعقل القضية الشرطية وهي مركبة من جملتين، والحكم بلزوم إحدى الجانبين الأخرى يستدعي حضور العلم بهما حال الحكم بذلك اللزوم، وذلك يدل على إمكان اجتماع العلمين دفعة في الذهن.
ومن الخامس: هب أن تلك الماهيات غير متصورة بحسب حقائقها لكنها متصورة بحسب عوارضها المشتركة بينها وبين المحدثات وذلك كافٍ في إمكان التصديق.
وعن السادس: أن ما ذكرتموه يدل على صعوبة تحصيل هذا العلم لا على تعذره.
Issue: The thought that yields knowledge exists.
The Samaniyya denied it absolutely, and a group of the engineers acknowledged it in arithmetic and geometry but denied it in divine matters, claiming that the ultimate aim in them is to adopt the most appropriate and fitting, but certainty has no path to it.
Our proof: Each of the two premises of the mentioned example is certain, and they may come together in the mind in a conjunction that entails the mentioned conclusion. Thus, the reasoning that yields knowledge exists.
The deniers of reasoning absolutely objected with four things:
First: The knowledge that the belief attained after reasoning is such that it cannot be necessary, for often the matter is revealed to be contrary to it, nor theoretical, otherwise circularity would follow, which is impossible.
Second: If the sought is known, then there is no benefit in seeking it; but if it is not known, then when he finds it, how does he know that it is his sought?
Third: A person may be insistent on the correctness of a proof for a long time, then later its weakness appears to him through a second proof, and that possibility remains in the second one, and with the possibility remaining certainty is not obtained.
Fourth: Knowledge of the two premises does not occur together in the mind, evidenced by the fact that we find in ourselves that when we direct the mind toward summoning one known thing we are able to direct it toward summoning another, so what is present in the mind at any moment is only knowledge of one premise, and that is not productive by agreement, so thought does not yield knowledge.
The deniers of reasoning in divine matters objected with two arguments:
First: The possibility of seeking assent depends on conceiving the subject and predicate, and divine realities are not conceivable. [Objector says:] We have what preceded: we only conceive what we find through our senses, our selves, or our intellects, and when the conception which is a condition of assent is absent, assent is also impossible.
The answer to the first: It is theoretical, and circularity is not entailed because if the entailment of the conclusion from the two premises is necessary, then they are both necessary, either immediately or through an intermediary with the same property, and he has necessary knowledge that what is entailed by the necessary is necessary. It is known necessarily that what is obtained is knowledge.
And to the second: It is known in conception but unknown in assent, and the sought is assent, so when he finds it he distinguishes it from others by the known conception.
And to the third: It is countered by errors of the senses.
And to the fourth: We may understand a conditional proposition, which is composed of two statements, and the judgment of the entailment of one side from the other requires the presence of knowledge of both at the time of judging that entailment, and that indicates the possibility of the two knowledges coming together in the mind at once.
And to the fifth: Suppose those essences are not conceivable in terms of their true realities, but they are conceivable in terms of their common accidents between them and the contingent things, and that is sufficient for the possibility of assent.
And to the sixth: What you have mentioned indicates the difficulty of obtaining this knowledge, not its impossibility.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-refutation sequence. It begins by stating the issue (mas'ala) that 'thought that yields knowledge exists'. The Samaniyya (a sect) denied it altogether, while some mathematicians acknowledged it in mathematics but denied it in theology, claiming that only plausible opinions are possible there, not certainty. The author then gives a brief proof: if two certain premises come together in the mind in a way that entails a certain conclusion, that process yields knowledge. Next, he lists four objections from absolute deniers of reasoning: (1) the belief after reasoning is neither necessary nor theoretical (to avoid circularity); (2) if the sought is known, seeking is futile, if unknown, one cannot recognize it; (3) a long-held proof may later be shown false, so doubt persists; (4) the mind cannot hold both premises simultaneously, so no inference occurs. Then two objections from deniers of theological reasoning: (5) divine essences are inconceivable, so assent is impossible; (6) even the self (the 'I') is disputed, how then can we know the hidden? The author then provides six answers in order: to (1) he distinguishes necessary entailment from circularity; to (2) he distinguishes conception from assent; to (3) he notes that sensory errors also occur but do not invalidate all sense perception; to (4) he gives the example of conditional propositions requiring simultaneous knowledge; to (5) he admits that divine essences are not directly conceivable but are known through their attributes common with contingent things; to (6) he says this only shows difficulty, not impossibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief that all reasoning must eventually lead to infinite regress.
Correction: The author will later show that necessary premises can end the regress.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection?
A) Reasoning leads to infinite regress.
B) If the sought is known, seeking is futile; if unknown, it cannot be recognized.
C) The mind cannot hold two premises at once.
D) Later refutation undermines certainty.

Key Takeaway
The four objections challenge reasoning on grounds of circularity, futility, uncertainty, and mental simultaneity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read objection two—that if you already know something, seeking is futile, and if you don't know it, you can't recognize it—does this reflect your own hesitation in studying proofs about Allah? Have you ever felt that you either already believe or you'll never be convinced? What does that feeling say about your trust in reasoning? Will you commit to seeking knowledge with the hope that you can recognize truth when it comes?
?
Objection three warns that a proof you hold for years can later be shown weak. Have you ever abandoned a belief because someone presented a seemingly stronger counter-argument? In that moment, did you realize that your certainty was fragile? What step will you take today to ground your faith in a proof that cannot be shaken, rather than just following what feels convincing?
?
The fourth objection says the mind cannot hold both premises of an argument at once, so inference is impossible. When you try to reflect on theological proofs, do you find your mind wandering or forgetting key steps? Does that make you doubt the validity of reasoning itself? On the Day of Judgment, will you excuse your ignorance by saying 'I couldn't hold two thoughts at once'? How will you train your mind to focus so that you can know your Creator with certainty?

Two Objections from Deniers of Theological Reasoning
Sanusi presents two objections from deniers of reasoning in divine matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific objections raised against using reasoning in divine matters.

Core Idea
Critics argue that divine essences are inconceivable, so reasoning about them is impossible.

In addition to the four general objections, some thinkers specifically deny reasoning in theology. They claim that divine realities cannot be conceived, making assent impossible. The author will later respond, but first we need to understand these objections clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that the first objection concerns inconceivability of divine essences, and the second concerns the unknowability of the self.
2
Analyze the first objection
The first objection argues that assent depends on conception, and divine essences are inconceivable because they are not sensed, self-evident, or rationally grasped.
3
Analyze the second objection
The second objection points to the difficulty of knowing the self, let alone higher realities, implying that theological knowledge is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the objection that divine essences are inconceivable, do you ever feel a moment of doubt? How does your heart respond to the challenge that reason may be insufficient in matters of faith? ?

In light of this lesson, are you ready to trust that divine revelation provides the necessary conception that reason alone cannot reach?
?
Sanusi presents these objections not as straw men but as real challenges. Do you have the courage to face the strongest arguments against theological reasoning? If you are honest, do you sometimes avoid difficult questions about the nature of God? This lesson calls you to engage, not to ignore. Will you commit to seeking knowledge through both reason and revelation?

Refutation of All Six Objections
Sanusi provides refutations for the six objections against beneficial reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes each of the six objections against beneficial reasoning.

Core Idea
Each objection is answered by showing that reasoning avoids circularity, is not futile, can yield certainty, and can handle simultaneous premises and divine concepts.

The author now provides systematic answers to the six objections. He addresses the four general objections first, then the two theological ones. These refutations are crucial for defending the validity of rational thought in all domains, including theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the six answers
Read the Arabic passage containing the six answers. Then read the translation. Note that each answer corresponds to the objection in order.
2
Understand each refutation
For each answer, identify the key point: (1) necessary premises avoid regress; (2) distinction between conception and assent; (3) sensory errors do not invalidate all sense; (4) conditional propositions require simultaneous knowledge; (5) divine essences known through attributes; (6) difficulty is not impossibility.
3
Assess the overall defense
Consider how the author's responses collectively defend the possibility of reasoning yielding knowledge, especially in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الفكر المفيد للعلم موجود
والسمنية أنكروه مطلقا، وجمع من المهندسين اعترفوا به في العدديات والهندسيات وأنكروه في الإلهيات، وزعموا أن المقصد الأقصى فيها الأخذ بالأولى والأليق، أما الجزم فلا سبيل إليه.
لنا: أن كل واحد من مقدمة المثال المذكورة يقيني وقد يجتمعان في الذهن اجتماعا مستلزما للنتيجة المذكورة، فالنظر المفيد للعلم موجود.
احتج المنكرون للنظر مطلقا بأمور أربعة:
أولها: العلم بأن الاعتقاد الحاصل عقيب النظر علم لا يجوز أن يكون ضروريا إذ كثيرا ينكشف الأمر بخلافه، ولا نظريا وإلا لزم التسلسل وهو محال.
ثانيها: أن المطلوب إن كان معلوما فلا فائدة في طلبه، وإلا فإذا وجده كيف يعرف أنه مطلوبه.
ثالثها: أن الإنسان قد يكون مصرا على صحة دليل زمانا مديدا ثم يظهر له بعد ذلك ضعفه بدليل ثان، وذلك الاحتمال قائم في ذلك الثاني، ومع قيام الاحتمال لا يحصل اليقين.
رابعها: أن العلم بالمقدمتين لا يحصل معا في الذهن بدليل أنا نجد في أنفسنا أنا متى وجهنا الذهن نحو استحضار معلوم نقدر عليه في تلك الحالة توجيهه نحو استحضار معلوم آخر، فالحاضر في الذهن أبدا ليس إلا العلم بمقدمة واحدة وذلك غير منتج بالاتفاق فالفكر لا يفيد العلم.
احتج المنكرون النظر في الإلهيات بوجهين:
أحدهما: أن إمكان طلب التصديق موقوف على تصور الموضوع والمحمول والحقائق الإلهية غير متصورة. لنا: ما سبق أنا لا نتصور إلا ما نجده بحواسنا أو نفوسنا أو عقولنا، وإذا فقد التصور الذي هو شرط التصديق امتنع التصديق أيضا.
الجواب عن الأول: أنه نظري والتسلسل عنه لازم، لأن لزوم النتيجة عن المقدمتين إذا كان ضروريا كانتا ضروريتين إما ابتداء أو بواسطة شأنها كذلك، وعنده علم ضروري بأن اللازم عن الضروري ضروري. علم بالضرورة أن الحاصل علم.
وعن الثاني: أنه معلوم التصور مجهول التصديق والمطلوب هو التصديق، فإذا وجده ميزه عن غيره بالتصور المعلوم.
وعن الثالث: أنه معارض بأغلاط الحس.
وعن الرابع: أنا قد نعقل القضية الشرطية وهي مركبة من جملتين، والحكم بلزوم إحدى الجانبين الأخرى يستدعي حضور العلم بهما حال الحكم بذلك اللزوم، وذلك يدل على إمكان اجتماع العلمين دفعة في الذهن.
ومن الخامس: هب أن تلك الماهيات غير متصورة بحسب حقائقها لكنها متصورة بحسب عوارضها المشتركة بينها وبين المحدثات وذلك كافٍ في إمكان التصديق.
وعن السادس: أن ما ذكرتموه يدل على صعوبة تحصيل هذا العلم لا على تعذره.
Issue: The thought that yields knowledge exists.
The Samaniyya denied it absolutely, and a group of the engineers acknowledged it in arithmetic and geometry but denied it in divine matters, claiming that the ultimate aim in them is to adopt the most appropriate and fitting, but certainty has no path to it.
Our proof: Each of the two premises of the mentioned example is certain, and they may come together in the mind in a conjunction that entails the mentioned conclusion. Thus, the reasoning that yields knowledge exists.
The deniers of reasoning absolutely objected with four things:
First: The knowledge that the belief attained after reasoning is such that it cannot be necessary, for often the matter is revealed to be contrary to it, nor theoretical, otherwise circularity would follow, which is impossible.
Second: If the sought is known, then there is no benefit in seeking it; but if it is not known, then when he finds it, how does he know that it is his sought?
Third: A person may be insistent on the correctness of a proof for a long time, then later its weakness appears to him through a second proof, and that possibility remains in the second one, and with the possibility remaining certainty is not obtained.
Fourth: Knowledge of the two premises does not occur together in the mind, evidenced by the fact that we find in ourselves that when we direct the mind toward summoning one known thing we are able to direct it toward summoning another, so what is present in the mind at any moment is only knowledge of one premise, and that is not productive by agreement, so thought does not yield knowledge.
The deniers of reasoning in divine matters objected with two arguments:
First: The possibility of seeking assent depends on conceiving the subject and predicate, and divine realities are not conceivable. [Objector says:] We have what preceded: we only conceive what we find through our senses, our selves, or our intellects, and when the conception which is a condition of assent is absent, assent is also impossible.
The answer to the first: It is theoretical, and circularity is not entailed because if the entailment of the conclusion from the two premises is necessary, then they are both necessary, either immediately or through an intermediary with the same property, and he has necessary knowledge that what is entailed by the necessary is necessary. It is known necessarily that what is obtained is knowledge.
And to the second: It is known in conception but unknown in assent, and the sought is assent, so when he finds it he distinguishes it from others by the known conception.
And to the third: It is countered by errors of the senses.
And to the fourth: We may understand a conditional proposition, which is composed of two statements, and the judgment of the entailment of one side from the other requires the presence of knowledge of both at the time of judging that entailment, and that indicates the possibility of the two knowledges coming together in the mind at once.
And to the fifth: Suppose those essences are not conceivable in terms of their true realities, but they are conceivable in terms of their common accidents between them and the contingent things, and that is sufficient for the possibility of assent.
And to the sixth: What you have mentioned indicates the difficulty of obtaining this knowledge, not its impossibility.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-refutation sequence. It begins by stating the issue (mas'ala) that 'thought that yields knowledge exists'. The Samaniyya (a sect) denied it altogether, while some mathematicians acknowledged it in mathematics but denied it in theology, claiming that only plausible opinions are possible there, not certainty. The author then gives a brief proof: if two certain premises come together in the mind in a way that entails a certain conclusion, that process yields knowledge. Next, he lists four objections from absolute deniers of reasoning: (1) the belief after reasoning is neither necessary nor theoretical (to avoid circularity); (2) if the sought is known, seeking is futile, if unknown, one cannot recognize it; (3) a long-held proof may later be shown false, so doubt persists; (4) the mind cannot hold both premises simultaneously, so no inference occurs. Then two objections from deniers of theological reasoning: (5) divine essences are inconceivable, so assent is impossible; (6) even the self (the 'I') is disputed, how then can we know the hidden? The author then provides six answers in order: to (1) he distinguishes necessary entailment from circularity; to (2) he distinguishes conception from assent; to (3) he notes that sensory errors also occur but do not invalidate all sense perception; to (4) he gives the example of conditional propositions requiring simultaneous knowledge; to (5) he admits that divine essences are not directly conceivable but are known through their attributes common with contingent things; to (6) he says this only shows difficulty, not impossibility.

Key Terms

الضروري
التصور
التصديق
القضية الشرطية
العوارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the first objection proves infinite regress is unavoidable.
Correction: The author shows that necessary premises can terminate the regress.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that the mind cannot hold two premises at once?
A) By denying the objection
B) By citing conditional propositions that require simultaneous knowledge
C) By appealing to divine grace
D) By saying it's rare but possible

Key Takeaway
All six objections are refuted, showing that reasoning can yield certain knowledge, even in theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a reasoned argument about Allah because it required an intermediate step? The first answer affirms that necessary knowledge can be mediated. Is your heart patient enough to follow a chain of reasoning, or do you demand immediate conclusions? Will you commit to studying proofs that require patience?
?
Consider the fourth answer: two knowledges can be present simultaneously. In your own search for certainty, do you allow multiple evidences to coexist in your heart, or do you insist on a single, simple proof? What does that say about your trust in the complexity Allah has placed in creation and revelation? Are you willing to embrace intellectual complexity for the sake of deeper faith?
?
The sixth refutation says difficulty does not equal impossibility. Think of an aspect of Islamic theology you find difficult to understand. Have you used that difficulty as an excuse to avoid seeking knowledge? What would change if you treated it as a challenge to overcome rather than a barrier? Will you take one concrete step today to study that topic despite its difficulty?

Breaking Circularity: The First Samaniyya Objection
Commentator summarizes the answer to the Samaniyya's first objection about circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessity of the conclusion from two necessary premises breaks the alleged circularity in reasoning.

Core Idea
Necessity from two necessary premises yields immediate necessary knowledge, breaking any infinite regress.

The Samaniyya argued that reasoning leads to an infinite regress. In this passage, the commentator summarizes the key response: the conclusion of a valid syllogism is necessarily entailed by its premises, and whatever is necessarily entailed by two necessary premises is itself known necessarily. This stops the regress because the necessity is immediate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises of the answer
Read the passage and find the two premises that together prove the conclusion is necessary.
2
Understand how the regress is broken
The conclusion is known intuitively from its conception, so no further syllogism is needed.
3
Reflect on the importance of necessary premises
The premises themselves are necessary, ensuring the conclusion is also necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: حاصل الجواب عن أول شبه السمنية أن العلم بأن نتيجة القياس المفروض علم نظري حاصل من مقدمتين إحداهما أن تلك النتيجة لازمة بالضرورة عن المقدمتين، وهذه المقدمة ظاهرة البيان كما ذكره في المنطق، وثانيتهما أن كل لازم بالضرورة عن ضروريتين علم بالضرورة، فإذا نتيجة القياس المفروض علم بالضرورة بديهي يحصل من نفس تصورها فينقطع التسلسل.
والجواب عن ثاني شبههم كما ذكر.
وأما الجواب عن ثالثها وهو المعارضة بغلط الحس: فالحاصل منه أن الحس يغلط مع أنكم معترفون بحكمه حقا فخلط العقل أيضا مثله، فإما أن يعترفوا بحقيقة أحكام العقل وإما أن ينكروا حقيقة أحكام الحس وهذا جواب جدلي.
والجواب الحق: أن وقوع الغلط في البعض مع جواز الاحتراز عنه لا يوجب رد الكل والاحتمال غير باق مع جزم العقل.
وعن رابعها وخامسها: كما ذكر.
I say: The summary of the answer to the first objection of the Samaniyya is that the knowledge that the result of the supposed syllogism is theoretical knowledge is obtained from two premises: one of them is that that result is necessarily entailed by the two premises, and this premise is clear as mentioned in logic; the second is that everything necessarily entailed by two necessary premises is known necessarily. Hence, the result of the supposed syllogism is known necessarily, intuitively, obtained from its very conception, so the circularity is broken.
And the answer to their second objection is as mentioned.
As for the answer to their third, which is the counter by sensory error: the gist of it is that the senses err while you acknowledge their judgment as true, so the error of the intellect is likewise. So either they acknowledge the truth of intellectual judgments or they deny the truth of sensory judgments, and this is a dialectical answer.
And the true answer: That the occurrence of error in some cases, while avoidance is possible, does not necessitate rejecting all, and the probability does not exist when the mind is certain.
And to their fourth and fifth: as mentioned.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a commentator's gloss on the previous responses, summarizing and clarifying the answers to the Samaniyya's first, third, and other objections. The commentator begins by summarizing the answer to the first objection (regarding circularity): the key is that the conclusion is necessarily entailed by two necessary premises, and whatever is necessarily entailed by two necessary premises is itself necessary. This breaks the potential circularity because the necessity is immediate from conception. For the second objection, he simply refers to the earlier answer. For the third objection (the possibility of error in reasoning is compared to sensory error), he explains two answers: a dialectical one (if you accept sensory judgments despite errors, you must accept intellectual judgments similarly) and a true answer (occasional error with possible avoidance does not invalidate all certainty, and when the mind is certain, probability does not remain). For the fourth and fifth objections (about simultaneous premises and inconceivable essences), he just refers to earlier answers.

Key Terms
النتيجة
الضروريتين
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the conclusion of the syllogism not lead to an infinite regress?
A) Because the conclusion is the same as the premises
B) Because the conclusion is known necessarily from its very conception
C) Because the premises are uncertain
D) Because the regress stops at the senses

Key Takeaway
The necessity of the conclusion from two necessary premises makes it immediately known, halting any infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Samaniyya argued that reasoning leads to an infinite regress of proofs. Have you ever felt that your faith in Allah requires an endless chain of reasons to be certain? What does this lesson say about the possibility of immediate, necessary knowledge that stops that chain? How will you seek that intuitive certainty today?
?
This passage teaches that a conclusion can be known necessarily without further proof if its premises are necessary. Reflect on a basic belief you hold about your Creator: do you treat it as merely theoretical, needing constant reinforcement, or as intuitively necessary? What concrete step can you take to move from the former to the latter?
?
The commentator says the conclusion is known 'from its very conception.' Are there truths about Allah, the Hereafter, or your own accountability that you have conceptually accepted but not felt as immediately necessary? If the circularity of doubt is broken only when knowledge becomes intuitive, what is holding you back from making that knowledge a felt certainty in your daily life?

Error in Reasoning or Senses? The Samaniyya's Third Objection
Commentator summarizes the answers to the Samaniyya's third objection about sensory error analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two answers to the objection that reasoning can be erroneous like the senses: a dialectical answer and a true answer.

Core Idea
Occasional error does not invalidate certainty when proper safeguards are taken.

The Samaniyya argued that since reasoning can err like the senses, we cannot trust it. The commentator presents two responses: a dialectical one that forces consistency, and a true one that distinguishes between occasional error and genuine certainty when safeguards are observed.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two answers
Identify the dialectical answer and the true answer in the passage.
Dialectical: consistency with their acceptance of sensory error. True: occasional error does not reject all.
2
Understand the dialectical response
If they accept sensory judgments despite sensory error, they must also accept intellectual judgments despite possible error.
3
Understand the true response
Error in some cases with possible avoidance does not necessitate rejecting all judgments, and when the mind is certain, no probability of error remains.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any possibility of error makes all reasoning unreliable.
