Razi's Three Arguments Against Non-Existent Realities
Razi enumerates three proofs against fixed non-existents and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's three arguments against the Mutazilite position that non-existent things have fixed reality, and how he responds to key objections.

Core Idea
Non-existent things cannot be counted, described, or be subjects of attributes.

Imam Razi challenges the Mutazilite concept of 'thubut' (fixed reality) for non-existent entities. He presents three arguments based on finitude, agency, and the logic of quiddity. This passage also includes responses to objections, showing the dialectical nature of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the main text of the arguments
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the three arguments presented by Razi.
The marginal notes (2)-(4) are objections and responses.
2
Identify each argument's structure
Break down each of the three arguments: first about countability and finitude, second about agency, third about quiddity and attributes.
Notice how each argument aims to show that non-existence cannot have fixed attributes.
3
Examine the objections and responses
Read the marginal notes (2)-(4) which are objections and Razi's responses. Understand how he refutes them.
These notes clarify potential counterarguments from the opponent.
4
Reflect on key terms
Consider the key terms like 'al-ma'dum', 'al-mahiyya', 'al-wahda', etc. How do they connect to the arguments?
These terms are central to the debate about non-existent entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موصوفة بها هذا خلف وعدد الذوات المعدودة قابل للزيادة والنقصان فيكون متناهيا والخصم لا يقول به ولأن الذوات أزلية فلا تكون مقدورة والوجوه حال عندهم فلا يكون مقدوراً عندهم، وإذا لم يقع الذات ولا الوجود بالفاعل كانت الذات الموجودة غنية عن الفاعل ولأن السواد المعدوم إما أن يكون واحداً أو كثيراً فإن كان واحداً فالوحدة إن كانت لازمة للماهية امتنع زوالها فوجب أن لا يتعدد في الوجود ، وإن لم تكن لازمة فيفرض ارتفاعها ، لأن كل ما كان ممكنا لا يلزم من فرض ارتفاعه محال ، فإذا زالت الوحدة حصل التعدد وهو لا يتحقق إلا إذا تباين الشيئان بالهوية ، ثم ما به التباين إن كان من لوازم الماهية فكل شيئين فهما مختلفان بالماهية هذا خلف، وإن لم يكن من لوازمها كان الشيء حال عدمه موردا للصفات المتزايلة ، ولو جاز ذلك الجاز أن يكون محل الحركات والسكنات المتعاقبة عدما محضا وذلك عين السفسطة
(۲) أقول : إنهم يقولون الزيادة والنقصان يقتضيان التناهى في الموجودات لا في المعدومات . (۳) أقول : هم يقولون جعل الذوات موصوفة بالوجود أمر زائد عليها كالتركيب الذي هو يدل على الأجزاء وهو بالفاعل ، ولا يلزم من كون الأفراد غنية كون المركب غنيا عنه (٤) أقول : لهم أن يقولوا السواد حالة العدم لا يوصف بالكثرة وأيضاً إن كان معدوداً فالتباين ليس من لوازمها، ولا يجب أن يكون كل ما يكون لازماً للماهية زايلا فلا يكون المعدوم مورداً للصفات المتزايلة والسفسطة غير لازمة .
It is absurd that it is described thereby. And because the number of countable essences is subject to increase and decrease, so it is finite, and the opponent does not affirm that. And because the essences are eternal, so they cannot be within power (maqdur), and the modes (wujuh) are states (hal) according to them, so they cannot be within power according to them. And when neither the essence nor existence is from an agent, the existent essence is independent of the agent. And because blackness when non-existent is either one or many. If it is one, then if oneness is necessary to the quiddity (mahiyya), its removal is impossible, so it must not become multiple in existence. And if it is not necessary, then its removal is assumed, because whatever is possible, its removal does not entail an impossibility. So when oneness is removed, multiplicity arises, and it is not realized except when the two things are distinct in identity. Then what distinguishes them, if it is among the necessary concomitants of the quiddity, then any two things are different in quiddity—this is absurd. And if it is not among its necessary concomitants, then the thing in its state of non-existence is a locus for alternating attributes. And if that were permissible, then it would be permissible for the locus of successive motions and rests to be pure non-existence, and that is sheer sophistry (safsata). (2) I say: They say that increase and decrease entail finitude in existents, not in non-existents. (3) I say: They say that making essences described by existence is something additional to them, like composition which indicates parts, and it is from an agent; and it does not follow from the individuals being independent that the composite is independent of it. (4) I say: They may say that blackness in the state of non-existence is not described with multiplicity; also if it is countable, distinction is not among its necessary concomitants; and it is not necessary that every necessary concomitant of the quiddity be removable, so the non-existent is not a locus for alternating attributes, and sophistry is not entailed.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments by Imam Razi against the Mutazilite position that non-existent things (ma'dumat) have some fixed reality (thubut). The first argument: If the number of essences can increase or decrease, they are finite, but the opponent denies finitude. Second: If essences are eternal, they are not subject to power, and the 'modes' (wujuh) are states (ahwal) which they also hold not subject to power, so existent essences need no agent. Third: An analysis of blackness (sawad) as non-existent: if it were one, its oneness would either be necessary to its quiddity (leading to impossibility of multiplicity) or not necessary (leading to it being a locus for alternating attributes, which is absurd). The marginal notes clarify possible objections: e.g., increase and decrease apply to existents, not non-existents; the agent acts on the composite, not the individuals; non-existents are not described with multiplicity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existent entities have some fixed reality (thubut) and can be counted or described with attributes.
Correction: Razi shows that this leads to logical contradictions such as finitude, lack of agency, and absurdities about quiddity and attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's third argument in this passage, if blackness (sawad) when non-existent is assumed to be one and oneness is not necessary to its quiddity, what follows?
A) Multiplicity arises necessarily.
B) The non-existent becomes a locus for alternating attributes.
C) The non-existent is impossible.
D) Blackness becomes multiple in existence.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the idea that non-existents have any fixed reality by showing it leads to absurdities like finitude, lack of agency, and problematic implications for quiddity and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your future and imagine different possibilities, do you treat those non-existent scenarios as though they have a fixed reality? How does Razi's argument challenge you to reconsider the nature of the non-existent and your dependence on the Creator?
?
Are there areas in your faith where you rely on your own understanding of essences, forgetting that all existence depends on the Agent (al-Fa'il)? How can you actively remind yourself of your absolute need for God in every moment?
?
In debates or disagreements, do you ever commit 'sophistry' by attributing real change or attributes to something that does not exist? How can you guard your reasoning from such fallacy and ensure your arguments are grounded in truth?

The Mu'tazilite Argument for the Reality of Non-Existents
Mu'tazila argue non-existents are distinct and fixed, with Razi's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite rational argument that non-existents are distinct entities and therefore have fixed quiddities, as well as Imam al-Razi's critical response.

Core Idea
Non-existents are known, willed, and acted upon — thus they must be distinct and have fixed reality.

The Mu'tazilites were known for their rationalist approach, and one of their most debated claims was that non-existents (ma'dum) have some kind of reality. In this passage, they present a structured argument to prove that non-existents are distinct entities. This argument relies on our ability to know, desire, and act upon things that do not yet exist. Let's examine their reasoning and the critical response from Imam al-Razi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and the translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the three proofs for distinctness.
The passage uses technical terms like 'المعدوم' (non-existent) and 'الثابت' (fixed).
2
Identify the three proofs
The Mu'tazila give three arguments for why non-existents are distinct: (1) knowledge, (2) capability, (3) will. Work through each one and see how they support distinctness.
Each proof shows a human relation to non-existents that requires distinction.
3
Understand the move from distinct to fixed
After establishing distinctness, they argue that distinctness implies fixedness because quiddities must be realized to be distinct. Consider why this step is controversial.
This is the key inference: distinction requires some mode of reality.
4
Consider Razi's critique
Imam al-Razi responds by saying the first argument is curtailed, and he offers a response that the Mu'tazila are insisting on fixedness while he assumes 'stripped of modes, not fixedness.' Understand his alternative view.
Razi introduces the concept of 'states' (ahwal) as a middle ground.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا بأمرين.
الحجة الأولى : المعدوم متميز وكل متميز ثابت فالمعدوم ثابت . بيان الأول من ثلاثة أوجه :
أحدها: أن المعدوم معلوم وكل معلوم متميز ، أما إن المعدوم معلوم فلان طلوع الشمس غداً معلوم الآن وهو معدوم ، والحركة التي يمكنني أن أفعلها كالحركة إلى اليمين والشمال والتي لا يمكنني أن أفعلها كالطيران إلى السماء معلومة مع أنها معدومة ، وأما أن المعلوم متميز فلاني أميز بين الحركة التي أقدر عليها والتي لا أقدر عليها وأميز بين طلوع الشمس من مغربها ومن مشرقها وكذلك أحكم على إحدى الحركتين بأنها توجد غدا وعلى الأخرى بأنها لا توجد ، ولا معنى للتميز إلا ذلك ثانيها : أني قادر على الحركة يمنة ويسرة وغير قادر على خلق السماء والأرض ، وهذا الامتياز حاصل قبل دخول هذه الأشياء في الوجود ، فلولا تميز بعض هذه المعدومات عن البعض وإلا لاستحال أن يقال إنه يصح من فعل هذا ولا يصح مني فعل ذلك
وثالثها : أن الواحد منا قد يريد شيئاً ويكره شيئاً آخر ، وإن كان المراد والمكروه بعد معدومين ولولا امتياز المراد عن المكروه قبل الوجود وإلا لاستحال أن يكون أحدهما مراداً والآخر مكروها ، فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة أن المعدومات الممكنة متميزة وأما أن كل متميز ثابت فلانا لا نعني بالثابت إلا كون هذه الماهيات في أنفسها متعينة ومتحققة ، ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أن امتياز هذه الماهية عن تلك الماهية لا يحصل إلا بعد تحقق هذه الماهية وتحقق تلك الأخرى ، فعلمنا أن هذه الماهيات متحققة حال العدم.
(1) أقول : أما الحجة الأولى فقد من الكلام فيها . وأما الثانية فإلزام اشتراك الثبوت حالة العدم فهم . معترفون به وقوله « فهي حال فأمر صناعي معراة عن صفة الثبوت، جوابه أنا فرضناها معراة عن الوجوه لا عن الثبوت ، ولا يقولون لما به الامتياز ثابتا بل إن كان ولابد فهي أحوال .
They argued with two things. The first argument: The non-existent is distinct (mutamayyiz) and everything distinct is fixed (thabit), so the non-existent is fixed. The explanation of the first is from three aspects: First: The non-existent is known (ma‘lum) and everything known is distinct. As for the non-existent being known, because the rising of the sun tomorrow is known now while it is non-existent, and the motion that I can perform like motion to the right and left, and what I cannot perform like flying to the sky, are known while they are non-existent. And as for the known being distinct, because I distinguish between the motion I am capable of and that which I am not capable of, and I distinguish between the rising of the sun from its west and from its east, and I judge that one of the two motions will exist tomorrow and the other will not exist, and there is no meaning of distinction other than that. Second: I am capable of motion to the right and left and incapable of creating the heavens and earth, and this distinction occurs before the entrance of these things into existence. For if some of these non-existents were not distinct from others, it would be impossible to say that it is sound for this one to be done or that it is not sound for me to do that. Third: One of us may will something and dislike something else, even though the willed and the disliked are still non-existent. And if there were no distinction between the willed and the disliked before existence, it would be impossible for one to be willed and the other disliked. So by these three aspects it is established that possible non-existents are distinct. And as for that every distinct is fixed, because we do not mean by 'fixed' except that these quiddities are in themselves determinate and realized (mutahaqqiqa). And it is known necessarily that the distinction of this quiddity from that quiddity does not occur except after the realization (tahaqquq) of this quiddity and the realization of that other. So we know that these quiddities are realized in the state of non-existence. (1) I say: As for the first argument, the discussion has been cut off concerning it. As for the second, it is an insistence on the commonality of fixedness (thubut) in the state of non-existence; they acknowledge it. And his statement 'So it is a state' is an artificial matter stripped of the attribute of fixedness. The answer: We assumed it stripped of modes (wujuh), not of fixedness. And they do not say that that by which there is distinction is fixed; rather, if and when, it is states (ahwal).
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of the Mutazilites for the reality of non-existents. They claim: The non-existent is distinct, and everything distinct is fixed, so the non-existent is fixed. They give three proofs for the premise that the non-existent is distinct: (1) Knowledge requires distinction; we know non-existents, so they are distinct. (2) Capability and incapability before existence imply distinction. (3) Will and dislike before existence imply distinction. Then they argue that distinction entails fixedness because the quiddities must be realized to be distinct. The marginal note by Razi comments that the first argument is curtailed, and the second argument forces recognition of common fixedness; he responds that we assume it stripped of modes, not fixedness, and the distinction is due to states (ahwal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since we can mentally distinguish non-existents (like future events), they must have some extra-mental reality or essence.
Correction: Mental distinction does not entail extra-mental reality. The Mu'tazila infer that distinction requires the quiddities to be realized in themselves, but critics (like Razi) argue that distinction can be based on 'states' (ahwal) or merely mental considerations.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof of the Mutazilites, why is the non-existent considered distinct?
A) Because it is willed and disliked.
B) Because it is known and what is known is distinct.
C) Because it is capable of being created.
D) Because it is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument for the reality of non-existents relies on three proofs of distinctness (knowledge, capability, will) and the claim that distinctness implies fixedness, but Imam al-Razi counters by introducing the concept of 'states' as a middle ground.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that your intentions, hopes, and future actions are non-existent right now. Yet you act in the present based on them. Does this lesson challenge you to treat your future possibilities as real and fixed in the sense that they are distinct and determined by your knowledge, capability, and will? What does that say about your responsibility to align your will with what is true?
?
When you differentiate between what you can and cannot do (like moving right vs. flying), do you acknowledge that this distinction is rooted in a fixed reality of your capability? Are there areas where you pretend you are capable of something you are not, or deny capability given by Allah? How will this passage change the way you assess your own power?
?
Imam al-Razi's response introduces 'states' (ahwal) as a middle ground between full reality and pure nothingness. Are there areas in your faith where you settle for a superficial understanding, avoiding the subtle truths that lie between extremes? Will you commit to seeking deeper knowledge even when it challenges comfortable dichotomies?

The Opponents' Second Argument: Distinction from the Impossible
The Mu'tazila argue that possibility must be a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that possibility is a positive attribute based on the nature of contradictories, and why they conclude that possible non-existents are fixed entities.

Core Idea
Possibility must be positive because impossibility cannot be.

This lesson presents the second argument of the Mu'tazila, who attempt to prove that possible non-existents are distinct and fixed. They rely on a logical principle about contradictories: one must be positive and the other privative. By showing that impossibility cannot be a positive attribute, they conclude that possibility must be positive, and thus possible non-existents have some fixed reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The argument is dense; it helps to read it aloud.
2
Understand the starting claim
The Mu'tazila assert that the possible non-existent is distinct from the impossible. This sets up the need for a distinction.
This claim is foundational to the entire argument.
3
Analyze the contradiction argument
Follow the logic: impossibility cannot be a positive attribute because that would make the impossible necessary. Then, since contradictories require one positive and one privative, possibility must be positive.
Pay attention to the logical structure: if A is false, then the opposite must be true.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
If possibility is a positive attribute, then what it describes (the possible non-existent) must be fixed. Consider why this conclusion is significant for the debate on non-existents.
This conclusion is used to argue for the reality of possible non-existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أن المعدوم الممكن متميز من الممتنع ولا يجوز أن يكون الامتناع وصفا ثبوتيا وإلا لكان الموصوف به ثابتا فيكون الممتنع الثبوت واجب الثبوت هذا خلف ، وإذا لم يكن الامتناع ثبوتيا كان الإمكان ثبوتيا ضرورة ؛ لأنه لابد في المتناقضين من كون أحدهما ثبوتيا والآخر سلبيا ، والموصوف بالوصف الثبوتي ثابت فالمعدوم الممكن ثابت .
The second argument: That the possible non-existent is distinct from the impossible, and it is not permissible for impossibility to be a positive attribute (wasf thubuti), for then the thing described by it would be fixed, so the impossible of existence would be necessary of existence—this is absurd. And if impossibility is not positive, then possibility must be positive of necessity, because in the case of two contradictories, one must be positive and the other privative. And what is described by a positive attribute is fixed, so the possible non-existent is fixed.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mutazilite argument. They claim that the possible non-existent (al-ma‘dum al-mumkin) is distinct from the impossible (al-mumtani‘). Impossibility cannot be a positive attribute because then the impossible would be fixed and thus necessary. Therefore, impossibility is a privative attribute. Since possibility is the contradictory of impossibility, possibility must be a positive attribute. And anything described by a positive attribute is fixed, so the possible non-existent is fixed. This classic argument forces the opponent to either accept that impossibility is positive (leading to impossible being necessary) or accept that possibility is positive and thus non-existents are fixed.

Key Terms
المعدوم الممكن
الممتنع
الامتناع
الإمكان
الوصف الثبوتي
الوصف السلبي
المتناقضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Impossibility is a positive attribute of the impossible.
Correction: If impossibility were positive, the impossible would be fixed and thus necessary, which is contradictory. Therefore impossibility cannot be positive.

Checkpoint
In the second argument, why must possibility be a positive attribute?
A) Because impossibility is a positive attribute.
B) Because possibility and impossibility are contradictories, so one must be positive.
C) Because possibility is known.
D) Because the possible is distinct.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila prove that possibility is a positive attribute, and thus possible non-existents have some fixed reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If possibility is a real positive attribute, then every possible action you could take but don't is still 'fixed' in some sense before God. How does that reality weigh on your heart when you consider the deeds you leave undone? Will you answer for them as well?
?
This argument proves that impossibility cannot be a positive description of anything. Are there areas of your life where you treat something as 'impossible' to change, and thereby deny God's power? Could it be that your 'impossibility' is merely a privation in your faith?
?
The Mu'tazila concluded that possible non-existents are fixed. If that is true, then God's knowledge of all possibilities is not abstract but real. Does your heart truly believe that God knows every possible you—every choice you could have made? And knowing that, are you grateful for the possibilities He actualized for you?

Razi's Four Counter-Arguments Against the Fixity of Non-Existence
Razi refutes the first argument with four counter-examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the Mutazilite claim that every non-existent is fixed by providing four counter-examples that show mental distinction does not entail external reality.

Core Idea
Mental distinction does not require extra-mental existence; even impossible things can be conceived.

Imam Razi challenges a key premise of the Mutazilite argument by denying that every non-existent is fixed. He then presents four compelling counter-examples that demonstrate how we can mentally distinguish things that do not and cannot exist outside the mind. This refutation undermines the claim that mental distinction implies external reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Familiarize yourself with the overall structure of the refutation.
Notice the clear enumeration: 'اولها', 'ثانيها', 'ثالثها', 'رابعها'.
2
Identify the four counter-arguments
Locate each of the four counter-examples. For each, note the specific thing that is mentally distinguished but not fixed in non-existence.
They are: partner of God, sea of mercury/mountain of ruby, existences of motions before existence, and composition/arrangement.
3
Understand the logic of each counter-argument
For each counter-argument, understand why the mental distinction does not require external existence. Razi shows that either the thing is impossible or it involves attributes that cannot exist in non-existence.
Engage with the reasoning: e.g., for the second, the composite quiddity is not fixed because components lack their accidents in non-existence.
4
Relate to the doctrine of al-tamayyuz al-dhihni
The concluding statement ('fathabita...') explicitly states that mental distinction does not demand realization of quiddities outside the mind. Absorb this as the lesson's key conclusion.
This directly refutes the Mutazilite premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول: لا نسلم أن كل معدوم ثابت ، والذي احتجوا عليه فهو معارض بأمور أربعة : أولها : أنا حكم على شريك الله تعالى بالامتناع ، ولولا أنا نتصوره متميزا عما عداه لاستحال الحكم عليه بالامتناع ، لأن ما لا يتصور لا يمكن الحكم عليه .
وثانيها : أنا نتصور بحرا من زئبق وجبلا من ياقوت ونحكم بامتياز بعض هذه المخيلات عن بعض مع أنها غير ثابتة في العدم؛ لأن الجبل من الياقوت عبارة عن أجسام قامت بها أعراض وعندكم ماهيات الجواهر والأعراض وإن كانت ثابتة في العدم لكن الجواهر غير موصوفة بالأعراض حال العدم فلا يمكن تقرير ماهية الجبل من حيث إنه جبل حال العدم.
وثالثها : أنا نتصور وجودات هذه الماهيات قبل دخولها في الوجود ونحكم بامتياز بعض تلك الوجودات من بعض فإني كما أعقل امتياز ماهية الحركة يمنة من ماهية الحركة يسرة قبل دخولهما في الوجود كذلك أعقل امتياز وجود إحدى الحركتين عن وجود الأخرى قبل دخولهما في الوجود ، فلو اقتضى العلم بامتياز الماهيات تحققها في العدم لاقتضى العلم بامتياز هذه الوجودات تحققها في العدم وذلك باطل بالاتفاق ، ولأن الوجود مناقض للعدم والجمع بينهما محال ورابعها : أنا تعقل ماهية التركيب والتأليف قبل دخولها في الوجود وهذه الماهية يمتنع تقريرها في العدم ؛ : لأن التأليف عبارة عن اجتماع الأجزاء وتماسها على وجه مخصوص ، وذلك يمتنع تقريره حال العدم بالاتفاق وإذا كان كذلك استحال أن يتقرر ماهية التأليف حال العدم، ثم إنا نتصورها قبل وجودها وتميز بينها وبين سائر الماهيات ، وكذلك نعقل المتحركية والساكنية قبل حصولهما مع أنهما من قبيل الأحوال ولا حصول لهما في العدم، فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن التميز الذهني لا يستدعي تحقق الماهيات خارج الذهن
(1) أقول : حاصل ما أورده من حججهم على أن المعدوم ثابت هو استدلالهم في الحجة الأولى بالتميز
And the answer to the first: We do not grant that every non-existent is fixed. What they argued for is opposed by four matters: First: We judge the partner of God Almighty as impossible, and if we did not conceive it as distinct from others, it would be impossible to judge it as impossible, because what is not conceived cannot be judged. Second: We conceive a sea of mercury and a mountain of ruby and judge some of these imaginings to be distinct from others, while they are not fixed in non-existence, because the mountain of ruby is an expression of bodies that have accidents, and according to you, the quiddities of substances and accidents are fixed in non-existence, but substances are not described by accidents in the state of non-existence, so it is impossible to establish the quiddity of the mountain as such in the state of non-existence. Third: We conceive the existences of these quiddities before their entrance into existence and judge some of those existences to be distinct from others, for just as I understand the distinction of the quiddity of motion to the right from the quiddity of motion to the left before their entrance into existence, so I understand the distinction of the existence of one motion from the existence of the other before their entrance into existence. So if knowledge of the distinction of quiddities required their realization in non-existence, then knowledge of the distinction of these existences would require their realization in non-existence, and that is false by agreement, and because existence contradicts non-existence and their combination is impossible. Fourth: We understand the quiddity of composition and arrangement before their entrance into existence, and this quiddity is impossible to be established in non-existence, because arrangement is an expression of the coming together of parts and their contact in a specific manner, and that is impossible to be established in the state of non-existence by agreement. And if that is so, it is impossible to establish the quiddity of arrangement in the state of non-existence. Then we conceive it before its existence and distinguish it from other quiddities. Likewise, we understand motion-ness and rest-ness before they occur, while they are of the category of states (ahwal) and have no occurrence in non-existence. So by these arguments it is established that mental distinction does not demand the realization of quiddities outside the mind. (1) I say: The sum of what he adduced as arguments that the non-existent is fixed is their inference in the first argument from distinction.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Razi's refutation of the Mutazilites' first argument. He begins by denying that every non-existent is fixed. Then he provides four counter-examples demonstrating that mental distinction does not entail extra-mental reality. (1) We conceive a partner of God and judge it impossible; this conception does not make it real. (2) We imagine a sea of mercury and a mountain of ruby—these are composed of substances and accidents that according to Mutazilites are fixed in non-existence, but the specific composite quiddity is not fixed because in non-existence substances are not described with accidents. (3) We conceive the existence of two motions before they exist; if distinction of quiddities required realization in non-existence, then distinction of their existences would also require realization, but existence is contradictory to non-existence. (4) We conceive composition (ta'lif) before existence, but composition requires parts in contact, which cannot exist in non-existence. Also, states like motion-ness and rest-ness are conceived before they exist, yet they have no reality in non-existence. Therefore, mental distinction (al-tamayyuz al-dhihni) does not require realization outside the mind. The marginal note summarizes the opponents' argument.

Key Terms

الثابت
التميز الذهني
الخارج
الماهية
التركيب
التأليف
المتحركية
الساكنية
الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental distinction of an idea proves its existence outside the mind.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that we can mentally distinguish things like a partner of God or imaginary composites that have no external reality.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of Razi's four counter-arguments?
A) The judgment that a partner of God is impossible requires conception of that partner.
B) Imagining a sea of mercury involves distinction of composites not fixed in non-existence.
C) The distinction of existences of motions before existence would require their non-existence.
D) The distinction of quiddities is impossible without external existence.

Key Takeaway
Mental distinction (al-tamayyuz al-dhihni) does not require quiddities to exist outside the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often judge things as impossible or possible in your mind. But does your mental certainty about someone's character or a future event require that your judgment corresponds to external reality? Razi's first argument shows that even judging something as impossible does not make it externally real. How many of your firm judgments about others are actually just mental constructs with no external basis? Will you examine them today?
?
Think of a worry that keeps you awake at night—some imagined disaster that you conceive clearly. Razi says you can conceive a sea of mercury, which has no reality. Your worry is like that: vividly conceived but not fixed in existence. Are you willing to treat that worry as a mere mental distinction and let it go? What would it take to live as if it has no external reality?
?
You mentally distinguish between 'good' and 'bad' versions of yourself, between what you 'could have been' and what you are. But Razi shows that mental distinction does not require external existence. The 'better version of you' you imagine does not need to have any reality to be conceived. How does this free you to stop comparing yourself to an imaginary ideal and instead engage with your actual existence?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
The Philosophers on Quiddities: Essence vs. Existence
Razi reports the philosophers' agreement that quiddities are not existence and can be considered without existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that quiddities are distinct from existence, can be considered without existence, are not inherently one or many, and are intrinsically intelligible.

Core Idea
Quiddities are what they are, independent of existence and attributes.

This lesson presents the core philosophical position on quiddities (mahiyyat). The philosophers argue that the essence of a thing is not the same as its existence, and that quiddities can be considered without existence, unity, or multiplicity. Understanding this distinction is foundational for later theological discussions on the nature of creation and the attributes of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the English translation. Notice that the passage presents several distinct claims.
Pay attention to the tone – Razi is reporting, not arguing.
2
Identify the four key theses
The passage contains four main agreements among the philosophers. List them: 1) Quiddities are not their existence. 2) They can be stripped of external existence (with a debate on mental existence). 3) They are not described as one or many. 4) They are not unknown.
These theses build on each other logically.
3
Examine the proof for intrinsic intelligibility
Focus on the last part: 'If the being of blackness as blackness were by another, then when that other ceases...' Work through the argument step by step to see why the philosophers conclude quiddities are self-sufficient.
This is a reductio ad absurdum proof.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this distinction matters: If quiddities depend on nothing else for their essence, then God's knowledge of them is possible without their existence. This underpins the philosophical theory of divine ideas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة فقد اتفقوا على أن الماهيات ماهياتها غير وجوداتها، واتفقوا على أنه يجوز تعري تلك الماهيات عن الوجود الخارجي، فإنا قد نعقل المثلث وإن لم يكن له وجود في الخارج، وهل يجوز تعريها عن الوجودين الخارجي والذهني؟ نص ابن سينا في المقالة الأولى من الطبيعيات الشفاء على أنه يجوز، ومنهم من لم يجوز. واتفقوا على أن تلك الماهيات لا توصف بأنها واحدة أو كثرة لأن المفهوم من الوحدة والكثرة مغاير للمفهوم من السواد، فإذا اعتبرنا السواد فقط ففي هذه الحالة لا يمكن الحكم عليها بالوحدة والكثرة وإلا فقد اعتبرنا مع السواد غيره وذلك يناقض قولنا إنا لم نعتبر إلا السواد فقط، بل الماهية مجردة عن الوحدة والكثرة. واتفقوا على أن الماهيات غير مجهولة. قالوا إن كل ما يجب بالغير يجب ارتفاعه عند ارتفاع ذلك الغير، فلو كان كون السواد سوادا بالغير لزم عند ارتفاع ذلك الغير أن لا يبقى السواد سوادا، لكن القول بأن السواد لا يبقى سوادا محال لأن المحكوم عليه هو السواد والمحكوم به أنه ليس بسواد والمحكوم عليه لا بد من تقرره عند حصول الحكم به فيلزم أن يكون سوادا حال ما لا يكون سوادا وهو محال.
As for the philosophers, they agreed that quiddities (mahiyyat) are not their existences, and they agreed that it is permissible to strip those quiddities of external existence, for we indeed conceive the triangle even if it has no existence in the external. And is it permissible to strip them of both external and mental existence? Ibn Sina in the first article of the Physics of al-Shifa' explicitly stated that it is permissible, and some of them did not permit it. And they agreed that those quiddities are not described as one or many, because the concept of unity and multiplicity is different from the concept of blackness. If we consider only blackness, then in this state it is impossible to judge it as one or many; otherwise we would have considered along with blackness something else, and that contradicts our statement that we considered only blackness. Rather, the quiddity is abstracted from unity and multiplicity. And they agreed that quiddities are not unknown. They said: Everything that is necessary by another must cease when that other ceases. So if the being of blackness as blackness were by another, then when that other ceases, blackness would cease to be blackness. But the statement that blackness does not remain blackness is impossible, because the subject of judgment is blackness, and the predicate is that it is not blackness, and the subject must be established when the judgment occurs, so it would follow that blackness is blackness at the time it is not blackness, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage reports the philosophers' (falasifa) position on quiddities (mahiyyat). First, they agree that quiddities are distinct from existence. Second, they allow stripping quiddities of external existence, using the triangle as an example: we conceive a triangle even if none exists externally. Ibn Sina permits stripping even mental existence, but some disagree. Third, quiddities are not inherently one or multiple because unity/multiplicity are concepts added to the quiddity. They illustrate with blackness: if we consider blackness alone, we cannot judge it as one or many because that would add another concept. Fourth, quiddities are not unknown (i.e., they are intelligible). The proof: if blackness's being blackness were due to another, then when that other ceases, blackness would cease to be blackness, which is contradictory; therefore blackness's essence is intrinsic, not derived.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the philosophers' argument proves that quiddities are separate entities that exist somewhere independently.
Correction: The argument does not posit a separate realm of entities; it shows that the essence of a thing is logically prior to and independent of its existence and attributes. Quiddities are not 'things' but the 'what-it-is' of things.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers say that quiddities cannot be described as one or many?
A) Because quiddities do not exist.
B) Because unity and multiplicity are not essential to the quiddity; they are added concepts.
C) Because quiddities are beyond logic.
D) Because quiddities are infinite.

Key Takeaway
Quiddities have an intrinsic reality that is not dependent on existence or accidents like unity; they are self-sufficient essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own identity—your 'you-ness'—do you believe it is dependent on your body, your reputation, or your achievements? The passage says quiddities are self-sufficient. What would it mean for you if your true essence were independent of everything external? Can you live as if that were true starting today?
?
The philosophers say we can conceive a triangle even if it has no external existence. Are there beliefs about yourself (like 'I am worthless' or 'I am a failure') that you hold in your mind but have no real existence? How can you strip them of mental existence and let them go?
?
Quiddities are not inherently one or many; unity is an added quality. In your life, do you attach labels like 'successful' or 'unsuccessful' to your core self? If you stripped away those labels, who would you be? Would that person be enough for you to face Allah with? Commit to one label you will detach from your essence this week.
